STATISTICAL APPENDIX

Definitions and Explanatory Notes

The following general notes supplement the footnotes given below the individual tables:

1. In an attempt to bring the material up-to-date provisional figures are included in some tables.

2. Figures in some tables have been rounded off to the nearest final digit. Hence there may be a slight discrepancy between the total as shown and the sum of its components.

3. Differences as compared with previously published figures are due to subsequent revisions.

4. The following symbols have been used throughout:-

n.a. = not available

— = nil

... = negligible

WEIGHTS AND MEASURES

Conversion Factors

British to Metric Units

1 acre = 0.405 hectares (ha)

1 pound (lb) = 0.454 kilogrammes (kgs) 1 long ton (2240 lbs) = 1.016 metric tons (mt. ton) 1 hundred weight (cwt) 50.802 kgs.

1 mile = 1.609 kilometres (kms) 1 long ton mile = 1.635 mt. ton kms

1 lb/acre 1.121 kgsVha 1 cwt/acre 125.536 kgsTha 1 pint 0.75 litres 1 imperial gallon = 4.55 litres

Metric to British Units

1 hectare = 2.471 acres 1 kilogramme 2.205 lbs 1 mt. ton (1,000 kgs.) 0.984 long ton 1 metre 3.281 feet 1 kilometre 0.621 mile 1 mt. ton kilometre = 0.612 long ton mile 1 litre = 1.76 pints = 0.219 imp. gallons 1 kgVha = 0.892 lb/acre

Paddy/Rice Conversions

1 bushel of paddy (46 lbs) = 20.87 kgs. 1 mt. ton paddy - 47.92 bushels paddy = 0.7 mt. ton rice 1 mt. ton rice = 68.46 bushels paddy = 1.43 mt. ton paddy 1 bushel paddy/acre = 51.55 kg. paddy/ha

entries in respect of such imports are in the merchandise account and are recorded on an arrivals basis. Debit & entries refer to a decline in such liabilities and are recorded at the time of settlement of payment for such 14.3 imports.

Items 15-20 The change in capital of the monetary sector is based on local records.

Item 21 The figures shown in the credit column relate to increase in liabilities to the I.M.F. arising from drawings from the I.M.F. Sri Lanka Rupee values reflect changes in the representative rate of the SDR. Figures in the debit column are in respect of repurchases or repayments, made in instalments to the I.M.F., in respect of earlier drawings. Subscriptions to the I.M.F., consequent to successive increases in Sri Lanka's quota, are also shown in the debit column.

(a) Excludes inter-bank deposits. (b) Commercial banks are required to maintain special reserves under a reserve tranche system against increase in deposits over the level as at 14th November 1984. Reserves in respect of each tranche are as follows.

Up to 3%-Nil Over 3% up to 6%-5% of the increase Over 6% up to 10%-10% of the increase, Over 10%-15% of the increase.

This requirement was terminated with effect from 28th February 1986.

(c) Under Sections 10c, 93,94,96 and 97 of the Monetary Law Act (Chapter422) Commercial banks are required to maintain reserves with Central Bank and the ratios so maintained shall not be less than 5 per centum or more than 20 per centum in the case of time and savings deposits and shall not be less than 10 per centum or more than 40 per centum in the case of demand deposits and unused balances of overdrafts allowed. (d) With effect from 9th August 1985, the reserves required to be held by the Commercial banks in respect of time and savings deposits were to take the form of rupee deposits in the Central Bank provided that not exceeding one eighth of such reserves may take the form of bonds issued by the Development Finance Corporation of Ceylon (DFCC) and held by such banks as an asset. With effect from 26th February, 1988 the reserves required to be held by the Commercial banks were to take the form of rupee deposits in the Central Bank provided that an amount not exceeding one-tenth of its required reserves in respect of the total of time and savings deposits may take the form of bonds issued by the DFCC and held by such banks as an asset.

With effect from 16th September, 1988 the amount of reserves that can be maintained in the form of Treasury Bills was increased to 5 per cent of the total deposits. With effect from 11th January 1991 the amount of reserves that can be maintained in the form of treasury bills was withdrawn.

With effect from 24th January, 1992, an amount of till cash over and above two per centum of the total of deposits liabilities but not exceeding four per centum may be maintained as a part of required reserves in the from of Sri Lanka currency notes and coins.

(e) Total till cash less the amount of till cash kept as reserves.

(f) Deposits liabilities have been defined to include foreign currency deposits as well with effect from 4th September, 1992.

(a) Th? weighted average yield rates on Treasury bills within the primary market for the month ending December, 1993 for 3 months, 6 months and 12 months were 18.09,18.47 and 19.38 per cent per annum respectively.

(b) This is the rate at which the Central bank grants advances to Commercial banks for their temporary liquidity purposes. However, since August, 1977 each bank, depending on selected assets items, was allocated a certain quota of such borrowings at the Bank rate. Borrowings in excess of this quota were subjected to a penal interest rate of 15 per cent per annum. In September, 1979 a graduated scale of penal rates ranging between 15 to 25 per cent was introduced. It was further revised upward to a range between 20 to 30 per cent effective from April, 1980. With effect from August, 1981 Bank rate was raised to 14 per cent, while the penal rates were raised to a range between 21 to 30. In March, 1983, the bank rate was brought down to 13 per cent and the penal rate was fixed at 20 per cent. With effect from 23rd November, 1983

the practice of granting penal rate advances to Commercial banks was discontinued. With effect from 30th December, 1985 the Central Bank reduced the lending rates on its advances to 11 per cent. With effect from 4th January, 1991 the Central Bank increased the lending rates on the advances to 17 per cent.
